<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Celts_and_Modern_Celts_sidebar" title="Template:Celts and Modern Celts sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Celts_and_Modern_Celts_sidebar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template talk:Celts and Modern Celts sidebar (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Celts_and_Modern_Celts_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Irish people</b> (<a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <span lang="ga"><i>Muintir na hÉireann</i></span> or <i>Na hÉireannaigh</i>) are a <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic group</a> native to the island of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, who share a common Irish ancestry, identity and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Ireland" title="Culture of Ireland">culture</a>. Ireland has been inhabited for about 9,000 years according to archaeological studies (see <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Ireland" title="Prehistoric Ireland">Prehistoric Ireland</a>). For most of Ireland's recorded history, the Irish have been primarily a <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> people (see <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic Ireland</a>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman" title="Anglo-Norman">Anglo-Normans</a> <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Ireland" title="Norman invasion of Ireland">conquered</a> parts of Ireland in the 12th century, while <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>'s 16th/17th century <a href="/wiki/Tudor_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Tudor conquest of Ireland">(re)conquest</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ireland" title="Plantation of Ireland">colonization</a> of Ireland brought a large number of <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Lowland</a> <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scots</a> to parts of the island, especially the north. Today, Ireland is made up of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> (an independent state), and the smaller <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> (a part of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>). The <a href="/wiki/People_of_Northern_Ireland" title="People of Northern Ireland">people of</a> Northern Ireland hold various national identities; including Irish, Northern Irish, British, or some combination thereof.</p>
<p>The Irish have their own customs, <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folk_music_of_Ireland" title="Folk music of Ireland">music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_dance" title="Irish dance">dance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_games" title="Gaelic games">sports</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_cuisine" title="Irish cuisine">cuisine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Irish_mythology" title="Irish mythology">mythology</a>. Although Irish (Gaelic) was their main language in the past, today most Irish people speak English. Historically, the Irish nation was made up of kin groups or <a href="/wiki/Irish_clans" title="Irish clans">clans</a>, and the Irish also had their own <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" title="Celtic polytheism">religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Early_Irish_law" title="Early Irish law">law code</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland#Dress" title="Gaelic Ireland">style of dress</a>.</p>
<p>There have been many notable Irish people throughout history. After Ireland's conversion to Christianity, Irish missionaries and scholars <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">exerted great influence</a> on Western Europe, and the Irish came to be seen as a nation of "saints and scholars". The 6th-century Irish monk and missionary <a href="/wiki/Columbanus" title="Columbanus">Columbanus</a> is regarded as one of the "fathers of Europe",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbanusZenit_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbanusZenit-14">[14]</a></sup> followed by saints <a href="/wiki/Saint_Kilian" title="Saint Kilian">Cillian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vergilius_of_Salzburg" title="Vergilius of Salzburg">Fergal</a>. The scientist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> is considered the "father of <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>", and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mallet" title="Robert Mallet">Robert Mallet</a> one of the "fathers of <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a>". Famous Irish writers include <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>, <a href="/wiki/W._B._Yeats" title="W. B. Yeats">W. B. Yeats</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bram_Stoker" title="Bram Stoker">Bram Stoker</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>. Notable Irish explorers include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brendan_the_Navigator" title="Brendan the Navigator">Brendan the Navigator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_McClure" title="Robert McClure">Robert McClure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Shackleton" title="Ernest Shackleton">Ernest Shackleton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Crean_(explorer)" title="Tom Crean (explorer)">Tom Crean</a>. By some accounts, the first European child born in North America had Irish descent on both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smiley630_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smiley630-15">[15]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">presidents of the United States</a> have had some Irish ancestry.</p>
<p>The population of Ireland is about 6.3 million, but it is estimated that 50 to 80 million people around the world have Irish forebears; making the <a href="/wiki/Irish_diaspora" title="Irish diaspora">Irish diaspora</a> one of the biggest of any nation. Historically, emigration from Ireland has been the result of conflict, famine and economic issues. People of Irish descent are found mainly in English-speaking countries, especially the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. There are also significant numbers in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. The United States has the most people of Irish descent, while in Australia those of Irish descent are a higher percentage of the population than in any other country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">Icelanders</a> have Irish and Scottish Gaelic forebears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_antecedents">Origins and antecedents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins and antecedents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_settlement_of_the_British_Isles" title="Prehistoric settlement of the British Isles">Prehistoric settlement of the British Isles</a></div>
<p>In its summary of their article 'Who were the Celts?' the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_Cardiff" title="National Museum Cardiff">National Museum Wales</a> note "It is possible that future genetic studies of ancient and modern human DNA may help to inform our understanding of the subject. However, early studies have, so far, tended to produce implausible conclusions from very small numbers of people and using outdated assumptions about linguistics and archaeology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amgueddfa_1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amgueddfa_1-18">[18]</a></sup> Nineteenth century anthropology studied the physical characteristics of Irish people in minute detail.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_and_legendary_ancestors">Prehistoric and legendary ancestors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Prehistoric and legendary ancestors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Ireland" title="Prehistoric Ireland">Prehistoric Ireland</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_history_of_Ireland" title="Early history of Ireland">Early history of Ireland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carrowmore_tomb,_Ireland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Carrowmore_tomb%2C_Ireland.jpg/220px-Carrowmore_tomb%2C_Ireland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Carrowmore_tomb%2C_Ireland.jpg/330px-Carrowmore_tomb%2C_Ireland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Carrowmore_tomb%2C_Ireland.jpg/440px-Carrowmore_tomb%2C_Ireland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carrowmore_tomb,_Ireland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Carrowmore" title="Carrowmore">Carrowmore</a> tomb, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 3000 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the past 10,000 years of inhabitation, Ireland has witnessed some different peoples arrive on its shores. The ancient peoples of Ireland—such as the creators of the <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9ide_Fields" title="Céide Fields">Céide Fields</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newgrange" title="Newgrange">Newgrange</a>—are almost unknown. Neither their languages nor terms they used to describe themselves have survived. As late as the middle centuries of the 1st millennium the inhabitants of Ireland did not appear to have a collective name for themselves.</p>
<p>Ireland itself was known by a number of different names, including <a href="/wiki/Banba" title="Banba">Banba</a>, <a href="/wiki/F%C3%B3dla" title="Fódla">Fódla</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89riu" title="Ériu">Ériu</a> by the islanders, <i>Iouerne</i> and <i>Hiverne</i> to the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hibernia" title="Hibernia">Hibernia</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> takes its name from Scota, who in Irish mythology, Scottish mythology, and pseudohistory, is the name given to two different mythological daughters of two different Egyptian Pharaohs to whom the Gaels traced their ancestry, allegedly explaining the name Scoti, applied by the Romans to Irish raiders, and later to the Irish invaders of <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caledonia" title="Caledonia">Caledonia</a> which became known as Scotland. Other <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> names for people from Ireland in Classic and Mediaeval sources include <a href="/wiki/Attacotti" title="Attacotti">Attacotti</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gael" title="Gael">Gael</a>. This last word, derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Welsh_language" title="Old Welsh language">Welsh</a> <i>gwyddel</i> "raiders", was eventually adopted by the Irish for themselves. However, as a term it is on a par with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Viking</a>, as it describes an activity (raiding, piracy) and its proponents, not their actual ethnic affiliations.</p>
<p>The terms <i>Irish</i> and <i>Ireland</i> are probably derived from the goddess <a href="/wiki/%C3%89riu" title="Ériu">Ériu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> A variety of historical ethnic groups have inhabited the island, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airgialla" title="Airgialla">Airgialla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fir_Ol_nEchmacht" title="Fir Ol nEchmacht">Fir Ol nEchmacht</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delbhna" title="Delbhna">Delbhna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fir_Bolg" title="Fir Bolg">Fir Bolg</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89rainn" title="Érainn">Érainn</a>, <a href="/wiki/E%C3%B3ganachta" title="Eóganachta">Eóganachta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mairtine" title="Mairtine">Mairtine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conmaicne" title="Conmaicne">Conmaicne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soghain" title="Soghain">Soghain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ulaid" title="Ulaid">Ulaid</a>. In the cases of the Conmaicne, Delbhna, and perhaps Érainn, it can be demonstrated that the tribe took their name from their chief deity, or in the case of the Ciannachta, Eóganachta, and possibly the Soghain, a deified ancestor. This practice is paralleled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> dynasties' claims of descent from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woden" title="Woden">Woden</a>, via his sons <a href="/wiki/Wecta" title="Wecta">Wecta</a>, Baeldaeg, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Casere" title="Casere">Casere</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wihtlaeg" title="Wihtlaeg">Wihtlaeg</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythographer" title="Mythographer">mythographer</a> <a href="/wiki/Euhemerus" title="Euhemerus">Euhemerus</a> originated the concept of <a href="/wiki/Euhemerism" title="Euhemerism">Euhemerism</a>, which treats mythological accounts as a reflection of actual historical events shaped by retelling and traditional mores. In the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">Icelandic</a> <a href="/wiki/Bard" title="Bard">bard</a> and historian <a href="/wiki/Snorri_Sturluson" title="Snorri Sturluson">Snorri Sturluson</a> proposed that the <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse</a> gods were originally historical war leaders and kings, who later became cult figures, eventually set into society as gods. This view is in agreement with Irish historians such <a href="/wiki/T._F._O%27Rahilly" title="T. F. O'Rahilly">T. F. O'Rahilly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_John_Byrne" title="Francis John Byrne">Francis John Byrne</a>; the early chapters of their respective books, <i>Early Irish history and mythology</i> (reprinted 2004) and <i>Irish Kings and High-Kings</i> (3rd revised edition, 2001), deal in depth with the origins and status of many Irish ancestral deities.</p>
<p>One legend states that the Irish were descended from one <a href="/wiki/M%C3%ADl_Esp%C3%A1ine" title="Míl Espáine">Míl Espáine</a>, whose sons supposedly conquered Ireland around 1000 BC or later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The character is almost certainly a mere personification of a supposed migration by a group or groups from <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberia</a> to Ireland. It is from this that the Irish were, as late as the 1800s, popularly known as "<a href="/wiki/Milesians_(Irish)" title="Milesians (Irish)">Milesian</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Medieval Irish historians, over the course of several centuries, created the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genealogical" title="Genealogical">genealogical</a> <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a> that all Irish were descendants of Míl, ignoring the fact that their own works demonstrated inhabitants in Ireland prior to his supposed arrival.</p>
<p>This <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrine</a> was adapted between the 10th and 12th centuries, as demonstrated in the works of <a href="/wiki/Eochaidh_Ua_Floinn" title="Eochaidh Ua Floinn">Eochaidh Ua Floinn</a> (936–1004); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flann_Mainistrech" title="Flann Mainistrech">Flann Mainistrech</a> (died 25 November 1056); Tanaide (died c. 1075) and <a href="/wiki/Gilla_C%C3%B3m%C3%A1in_mac_Gilla_Samthainde" title="Gilla Cómáin mac Gilla Samthainde">Gilla Cómáin mac Gilla Samthainde</a> (fl. 1072). Many of their compositions were incorporated into the compendium <a href="/wiki/Lebor_Gab%C3%A1la_%C3%89renn" title="Lebor Gabála Érenn">Lebor Gabála Érenn</a>.</p>
<p>This tradition was enhanced and embedded in the tradition by successive historians such as <a href="/wiki/Dubs%C3%BAilech_%C3%93_Maolconaire" title="Dubsúilech Ó Maolconaire">Dubsúilech Ó Maolconaire</a> (died 1270); <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_M%C3%B3r_%C3%93_Dubhag%C3%A1in" title="Seán Mór Ó Dubhagáin">Seán Mór Ó Dubhagáin</a> (d.1372); <a href="/wiki/Giolla_%C3%8Dosa_M%C3%B3r_Mac_Fir_Bhisigh" title="Giolla Íosa Mór Mac Fir Bhisigh">Giolla Íosa Mór Mac Fir Bhisigh</a> (fl. 1390–1418); <a href="/wiki/Pilip_Ballach_%C3%93_Duibhgeann%C3%A1in" title="Pilip Ballach Ó Duibhgeannáin">Pilip Ballach Ó Duibhgeannáin</a> (fl. 1579–1590) and Flann Mac Aodhagáin (alive 1640). The first Irish historian who questioned the reliability of such accounts was <a href="/wiki/Dubhaltach_Mac_Fhirbhisigh" title="Dubhaltach Mac Fhirbhisigh">Dubhaltach Mac Fhirbhisigh</a> (murdered 1671).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_Europe" title="Genetic history of Europe">Genetic history of Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Y-DNA_haplogroups_in_European_populations" title="Y-DNA haplogroups in European populations">Y-DNA haplogroups in European populations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_the_British_Isles" title="Genetic history of the British Isles">Genetic history of the British Isles</a></div>
<p>Genetic research shows a strong similarity between the <a href="/wiki/Y_chromosome" title="Y chromosome">Y chromosome</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplotypes" title="Haplotypes">haplotypes</a> of males from Basque country and Irish men with <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Gaelic</a> surnames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The frequency of Y-DNA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R1b_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup R1b (Y-DNA)">haplogroup R1b</a> (the most common haplogroup in Europe) is highest in the populations of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Europe" title="Atlantic Europe">Atlantic Europe</a> and, due to European emigration, in North America, South America, and Australia. In Ireland and the <a href="/wiki/Basque_Country_(greater_region)" title="Basque Country (greater region)">Basque Country</a> its frequency exceeds 90% and approaches 100% in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Ireland" title="Western Ireland">Western Ireland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The incidence of R1b is 70% or more in Celtic regions – <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> in England, the Celtic <a href="/wiki/Norte_Region,_Portugal" title="Norte Region, Portugal">Northern</a> region in Portugal (<a href="/wiki/Douro_Litoral_Province" title="Douro Litoral Province">Douro Litoral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minho_Province" title="Minho Province">Minho</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%A1s-os-Montes_e_Alto_Douro_Province" title="Trás-os-Montes e Alto Douro Province">Trás-os-Montes e Alto Douro</a>), northern <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> (Celtic <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">Asturias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n_(historical_region)" title="León (historical region)">León</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cantabria" title="Cantabria">Cantabria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basque_Country_(greater_region)" title="Basque Country (greater region)">Basque Country</a>), western France (<a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9arn" title="Béarn">Béarn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gascony" title="Gascony">Gascony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guyenne" title="Guyenne">Guyenne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saintonge" title="Saintonge">Saintonge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angoumois" title="Angoumois">Angoumois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aunis" title="Aunis">Aunis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poitou" title="Poitou">Poitou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Touraine" title="Touraine">Touraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anjou" title="Anjou">Anjou</a> and the Celtic <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>), and Celtic Countries – <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> in Britain. R1b's incidence declines gradually with distance from these areas but it is still common across the central areas of Europe. R1b is the most frequent haplogroup in Germany and in the Low Countries, and is common in southern <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and in northern and central <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. This led to writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Oppenheimer" title="Stephen Oppenheimer">Stephen Oppenheimer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Sykes" title="Bryan Sykes">Bryan Sykes</a>, to conclude that the majority of Irish people primarily descend from an "Iberian refugium" <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">population bottleneck</a> dating back to the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, this haplogroup is now believed by some to have originated over 12,000 years more recently than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> It thus follows that Irish and many other R1b <a href="/wiki/Subclade" title="Subclade">subclades</a> will be considerably younger than the maximum age of 18,000 years. The previous estimates, based on inaccurate dating methods (30,000+ years BP), made R1b and its subclades seem to be more useful indicators of the paleolithic era populations of western Europe than they actually are. According to recent 2009 studies by Bramanti et al. and Malmström et al. on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MtDNA" title="MtDNA">mtDNA</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencemag_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencemag-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> related western European populations appear to be largely from the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">neolithic</a> and not <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">paleolithic</a> era, as previously thought. There was discontinuity between <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">mesolithic</a> central Europe and modern European populations mainly due to an extremely high frequency of haplogroup U (particularly U5) types in mesolithic central European sites.</p>
<p>The existence of an especially strong genetic association between the Irish and the Basques, one even closer than the relationship between other west Europeans, was first challenged in 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and in 2007 scientists began looking at the possibility of a more recent Mesolithic- or even Neolithic-era entrance of R1b into Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> A new study published in 2010 by Balaresque et al. implies either a Mesolithic- or Neolithic- (not Paleolithic) era entrance of R1b into Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balaresque2010_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balaresque2010-31">[31]</a></sup> However, all these genetic studies are in agreement that the Irish and Basque (along with the Welsh) share the highest percentage of R1b populations. A recent whole genome analysis of 4 neolithic and <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> skeletal remains in Ireland suggested that the original farming population was a mixture descended from Middle Eastern/southwest Mediterranean and eastern European immigrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_Irish">Black Irish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Black Irish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">"Black Irish" redirects here. For Irish people of black African descent, see <a href="/wiki/Black_people_in_Ireland" title="Black people in Ireland">Black people in Ireland</a>.</div>
<p><i>Black Irish</i> is an ambiguous term sometimes used (mainly outside Ireland) as a reference to a dark-haired <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> appearing in people of Irish origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> However, dark hair in people of Irish descent is common, although darker skin complexions appear less frequently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> One popular speculation suggests the Black Irish are descendants of survivors of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a>, despite research discrediting such claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bob_Quinn_(Irish_filmmaker)" title="Bob Quinn (Irish filmmaker)">Bob Quinn</a>'s documentary series <a href="/wiki/Atlantean_(documentary_series)" title="Atlantean (documentary series)"><i>Atlantean</i></a>, he explores an alternative 'Iberian' hypothesis, proposing the existence of an ancient sea-trading route skirting the Atlantic coast from North Africa and the Iberian Peninsula to regions such as <a href="/wiki/Connemara" title="Connemara">Connemara</a>. While preferring the term "The Atlantean Irish", Quinn's reference to certain phenotypical characteristics (within elements of the Irish populace and diaspora) as possible evidence of a previous Hibernian-Iberian (and possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a>) admixture mirrors common descriptions of the Black Irish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term has also come to be used to refer to the African-Irish descendants of those who live on <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montserrat" title="Montserrat">Montserrat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> a number of whom have Irish surnames, still retain part of their Irish accents and sing bilingual songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_expansion_and_the_coming_of_Christianity">Early expansion and the coming of Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early expansion and the coming of Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_history_of_Ireland" title="Early history of Ireland">Early history of Ireland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clonard_RC_Church_St_Finian_07_Detail_2007_08_26.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="892" data-file-width="621" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Clonard_RC_Church_St_Finian_07_Detail_2007_08_26.jpg/220px-Clonard_RC_Church_St_Finian_07_Detail_2007_08_26.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Clonard_RC_Church_St_Finian_07_Detail_2007_08_26.jpg/330px-Clonard_RC_Church_St_Finian_07_Detail_2007_08_26.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Clonard_RC_Church_St_Finian_07_Detail_2007_08_26.jpg/440px-Clonard_RC_Church_St_Finian_07_Detail_2007_08_26.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clonard_RC_Church_St_Finian_07_Detail_2007_08_26.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Finnian_of_Clonard" title="Finnian of Clonard">Finnian of Clonard</a> imparting his blessing to the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles_of_Ireland" title="Twelve Apostles of Ireland">Twelve Apostles of Ireland</a>"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One Roman historian records that the Irish people were divided into "sixteen different nations" or tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacManus86_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacManus86-40">[40]</a></sup> Traditional histories assert that the Romans never attempted to conquer Ireland, although it may have been considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacManus86_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacManus86-40">[40]</a></sup> The Irish were not, however, cut off from Europe; they frequently raided the Roman territories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacManus86_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacManus86-40">[40]</a></sup> and also maintained trade links.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the most famous people of ancient Irish history are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Kings_of_Ireland" title="High Kings of Ireland">High Kings of Ireland</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Cormac_mac_Airt" title="Cormac mac Airt">Cormac mac Airt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niall_of_the_Nine_Hostages" title="Niall of the Nine Hostages">Niall of the Nine Hostages</a>, and the semi-legendary <a href="/wiki/Fianna" title="Fianna">Fianna</a>. The 20th-century writer <a href="/wiki/Seumas_MacManus" title="Seumas MacManus">Seumas MacManus</a> wrote that even if the Fianna and the <a href="/wiki/Fenian_Cycle" title="Fenian Cycle">Fenian Cycle</a> were purely fictional, it would still be representative of the character of the Irish people:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...such beautiful fictions of such beautiful ideals, by themselves presume and prove beautiful-souled people, capable of appreciating lofty ideals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The introduction of Christianity to the Irish people during the 5th century brought a radical change to the Irish people's foreign relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacManus89_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacManus89-43">[43]</a></sup> The only military raid abroad recorded after that century is a presumed invasion of <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, which according to a Welsh manuscript may have taken place around the 7th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacManus89_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacManus89-43">[43]</a></sup> In the words of Seumas MacManus:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>If we compare the history of Ireland in the 6th century, after Christianity was received, with that of the 4th century, before the coming of Christianity, the wonderful change and contrast is probably more striking than any other such change in any other nation known to history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacManus89_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacManus89-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Following the conversion of the Irish to Christianity, Irish secular laws and social institutions remained in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_and_invasion_in_the_Middle_Ages">Migration and invasion in the Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Migration and invasion in the Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Medieval_Ireland_800%E2%80%931166" title="Early Medieval Ireland 800–1166">Early Medieval Ireland 800–1166</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Ireland" title="Norman invasion of Ireland">Norman invasion of Ireland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dalriada.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1123" data-file-width="794" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dalriada.png/220px-Dalriada.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dalriada.png/330px-Dalriada.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dalriada.png/440px-Dalriada.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dalriada.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The approximate area of the <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="Dál Riata">Dál Riata</a> (shaded)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 'traditional' view is that, in the 4th or 5th century, Gaelic language and culture was brought to Scotland by settlers from Ireland, who founded the Gaelic kingdom of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="Dál Riata">Dál Riata</a> on Scotland's west coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> This is based mostly on medieval writings from the 9th and 10th centuries. The archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Ewan_Campbell" title="Ewan Campbell">Ewan Campbell</a> argues against this view, saying that there is no archeological or placename evidence for a migration or a takeover by a small group of elites. He states that "the Irish migration hypothesis seems to be a classic case of long-held historical beliefs influencing not only the interpretation of documentary sources themselves, but the subsequent invasion paradigm being accepted uncritically in the related disciplines of archaeology and linguistics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ewancampbell_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-ewancampbell-47">[47]</a></sup> Dál Riata and the territory of the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a> merged to form the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a>, and Gaelic language and culture became dominant there. The country came to be called <i>Scotland</i>, after the Roman name for the Gaels: <i><a href="/wiki/Scoti" title="Scoti">Scoti</a></i>. The <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Manx_people" title="Manx people">Manx people</a> also came under massive Gaelic influence in their history.</p>
<p>Irish missionaries such as <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Saint Columba</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">Christianity to Pictish Scotland</a>. The Irishmen of this time were also "aware of the cultural unity of Europe", and it was the 6th-century Irish monk <a href="/wiki/Columbanus" title="Columbanus">Columbanus</a> who is regarded as "one of the fathers of Europe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbanusZenit_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbanusZenit-14">[14]</a></sup> Another Irish saint, <a href="/wiki/Aidan_of_Lindisfarne" title="Aidan of Lindisfarne">Aidan of Lindisfarne</a>, has been proposed as a possible <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of the United Kingdom,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Saint_Kilian" title="Saint Kilian">Saints Kilian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vergilius_of_Salzburg" title="Vergilius of Salzburg">Vergilius</a> became the patron saints of <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrzburg" title="Würzburg">Würzburg</a> in Germany and <a href="/wiki/Salzburg" title="Salzburg">Salzburg</a> in Austria, respectively. Irish missionaries founded monasteries outside Ireland, such as <a href="/wiki/Iona_Abbey" title="Iona Abbey">Iona Abbey</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbey_of_St_Gall" title="Abbey of St Gall">Abbey of St Gall</a> in Switzerland, and <a href="/wiki/Bobbio_Abbey" title="Bobbio Abbey">Bobbio Abbey</a> in Italy.</p>
<p>Common to both the monastic and the secular bardic schools were Irish and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>. With Latin, the early Irish scholars "show almost a like familiarity that they do with their own Gaelic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> There is evidence also that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> were studied, the latter probably being taught at Iona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"The knowledge of Greek", says Professor Sandys in his History of Classical Scholarship, "which had almost vanished in the west was so widely dispersed in the schools of Ireland that if anyone knew Greek it was assumed he must have come from that country."'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Since the time of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, Irish scholars had a considerable presence in the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Frankish court</a>, where they were renowned for their learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEriugena_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEriugena-52">[52]</a></sup> The most significant Irish intellectual of the early monastic period was the 9th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johannes_Scotus_Eriugena" title="Johannes Scotus Eriugena">Johannes Scotus Eriugena</a>, an outstanding philosopher in terms of originality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEriugena_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEriugena-52">[52]</a></sup> He was the earliest of the founders of <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a>, the dominant school of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">medieval philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gothic_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gothic-53">[53]</a></sup> He had considerable familiarity with the Greek language, and translated many works into Latin, affording access to the <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Fathers" title="Cappadocian Fathers">Cappadocian Fathers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Christian_theology" title="Eastern Orthodox Christian theology">Greek theological tradition</a>, previously almost unknown in the Latin West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordEriugena_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEriugena-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The influx of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Viking</a> raiders and traders in the 9th and 10th centuries resulted in the founding of many of Ireland's most important towns, including <a href="/wiki/Cork_(city)" title="Cork (city)">Cork</a>, Dublin, <a href="/wiki/Limerick" title="Limerick">Limerick</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Waterford" title="Waterford">Waterford</a> (earlier Gaelic settlements on these sites did not approach the urban nature of the subsequent Norse trading ports). The Vikings left little impact on Ireland other than towns and certain words added to the Irish language, but many Irish taken as slaves inter-married with the Scandinavians, hence forming a close link with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Icelandic_people" title="Icelandic people">Icelandic people</a>. In the Icelandic <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laxd%C5%93la_saga" title="Laxdœla saga">Laxdœla saga</a></i>, for example, "even slaves are highborn, descended from the kings of Ireland."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The first name of <a href="/wiki/Nj%C3%A1ll_%C3%9Eorgeirsson" title="Njáll Þorgeirsson">Njáll Þorgeirsson</a>, the chief protagonist of <i><a href="/wiki/Nj%C3%A1ls_saga" title="Njáls saga">Njáls saga</a></i>, is a variation of the Irish name <a href="/wiki/Neil" title="Neil">Neil</a>. According to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eirik_the_Red%27s_Saga" title="Eirik the Red's Saga">Eirik the Red's Saga</a></i>, the first European couple to have a child born in North America was descended from the Viking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kings_of_Dublin" title="Kings of Dublin">Queen of Dublin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aud_the_Deep-minded" title="Aud the Deep-minded">Aud the Deep-minded</a>, and a Gaelic slave brought to Iceland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smiley630_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smiley630-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16th-century-Irish.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="729" data-file-width="525" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/16th-century-Irish.jpg/220px-16th-century-Irish.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/16th-century-Irish.jpg/330px-16th-century-Irish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/16th-century-Irish.jpg/440px-16th-century-Irish.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:16th-century-Irish.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Irish Gaels in a painting from the 16th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Anglo-Normans</a> brought also the <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flemish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bretons" title="Bretons">Bretons</a>. Most of these were <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilated</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_culture" title="Irish culture">Irish culture</a> and polity by the 15th century, with the exception of some of the walled towns and <a href="/wiki/The_Pale" title="The Pale">the Pale</a> areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a> also saw the settlement of Scottish <a href="/wiki/Gallowglass" title="Gallowglass">gallowglass</a> families of mixed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaelic-Norse" title="Gaelic-Norse">Gaelic-Norse</a> <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">-Pict</a> descent, mainly in the north; due to similarities of language and culture they too were assimilated.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surnames">Surnames</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Surnames">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irish_name" title="Irish name">Irish name</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_onomastics" title="Celtic onomastics">Celtic onomastics</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(February 2010)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Irish were among the first people in Europe to use surnames as we know them today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> It is very common for people of <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> origin to have the English versions of their surnames beginning with "O'" or "Mc" (less frequently "Mac" and occasionally shortened to just "Ma" at the beginning of the name).</p>
<p>"O'" comes from the Gaelic Ó which in turn came from Ua, which means "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grandson_(son_of_a_child)" title="Grandson (son of a child)">grandson</a>", or "<a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">descendant</a>" of a named person. Names that begin with "O'" include Ó Bánion (<a href="/wiki/O%27Banion" title="O'Banion">O'Banion</a>), Ó Briain (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O%27Brien_(surname)" title="O'Brien (surname)">O'Brien</a>), Ó Cheallaigh (<a href="/wiki/Kelly_(surname)" title="Kelly (surname)">O'Kelly</a>), Ó Conchobhair (<a href="/wiki/O%27Connor" title="O'Connor">O'Connor</a>), Ó Chonaill (<a href="/wiki/O%27Connell_(name)" title="O'Connell (name)">O'Connell</a>), O'Coiligh (<a href="/wiki/Cox_(surname)" title="Cox (surname)">Cox</a>), Ó Cuilinn (<a href="/wiki/Cullen_(surname)" title="Cullen (surname)">Cullen</a>), Ó Domhnaill (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O%27Donnell" title="O'Donnell">O'Donnell</a>), Ó Máille (<a href="/wiki/O%27Malley_(surname)" title="O'Malley (surname)">O'Malley</a>), Ó Mathghamhna (<a href="/wiki/O%27Mahony" title="O'Mahony">O'Mahony</a>), Ó Néill (<a href="/wiki/O%27Neill_(surname)" title="O'Neill (surname)">O'Neill</a>), Ó Sé (<a href="/wiki/O%27Shea" title="O'Shea">O'Shea</a>), Ó Súilleabháin (<a href="/wiki/O%27Sullivan" title="O'Sullivan">O'Sullivan</a>), and Ó Tuathail (<a href="/wiki/O%27Toole_(surname)" title="O'Toole (surname)">O'Toole</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>"Mac" or "Mc" means "son". Names that begin with Mac or Mc include Mac Cárthaigh (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McCarthy_(surname)" title="McCarthy (surname)">MacCarthy</a>), Mac Diarmada (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDermott" title="McDermott">MacDermott</a>), Mac Domhnaill (<a href="/wiki/McDonnell_(surname)" title="McDonnell (surname)">MacDonnell</a>), and Mac Mathghamhna (<a href="/wiki/McMahon" title="McMahon">MacMahon</a>). Mac is commonly anglicised Mc. However, "Mac" and "Mc" are not mutually exclusive, so, for example, both "MacCarthy" and "McCarthy" are used. While both "Mac" and "O'" prefixes are Gaelic in origin, "Mac" is more common in Scotland and in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> than in the rest of Ireland; furthermore, "Ó" is far less common in Scotland than it is in Ireland. The proper surname for a woman in Irish uses the feminine prefix nic (meaning daughter) in place of mac. Thus a boy may be called Mac Domhnaill whereas his sister would be called Nic Dhomhnaill or Ní Dhomhnaill – the insertion of 'h' follows the female prefix in the case of most consonants (bar H, L, N, R, &amp; T).</p>
<p>A son has the same surname as his father. A female's surname replaces Ó with Ní (reduced from Iníon Uí – "daughter of the grandson of") and Mac with Nic (reduced from Iníon Mhic – "daughter of the son of"); in both cases the following name undergoes lenition. However, if the second part of the surname begins with the letter C or G, it is not lenited after Nic.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Thus the daughter of a man named Ó Maolagáin has the surname <i>Ní Mhaolagáin</i> and the daughter of a man named Mac Gearailt has the surname <i>Nic Gearailt</i>. When anglicised, the name can remain O' or Mac, regardless of gender.</p>
<p>There are a number of Irish surnames derived from Norse personal names, including <a href="/wiki/Clan_Sweeney" title="Clan Sweeney">Mac Suibhne</a> (Sweeney) from Swein and McAuliffe from "Olaf". The name <a href="/wiki/Cotter_family" title="Cotter family">Cotter</a>, local to <a href="/wiki/County_Cork" title="County Cork">County Cork</a>, derives from the Norse personal name Ottir. The name <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_(surname)" title="Reynolds (surname)">Reynolds</a> is an Anglicization of the Gaelic Mac Raghnaill, itself originating from the Norse names Randal or Reginald. Though these names were of Viking derivation some of the families who bear them appear to have had Gaelic origins.</p>
<p>"Fitz" is an old Norman French variant of the Old French word <i>fils</i> (variant spellings filz, fiuz, fiz, etc.), used by the Normans, meaning <i>son</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a> themselves were descendants of <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a>, who had settled in <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a> and thoroughly adopted the French language and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> With the exception of the Gaelic-Irish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fitzpatrick_(name)" title="Fitzpatrick (name)">Fitzpatrick</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mac_Giolla_Ph%C3%A1draig" title="Mac Giolla Phádraig">Mac Giolla Phádraig</a>) surname, all names that begin with Fitz – including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FitzGerald" title="FitzGerald">FitzGerald</a> (Mac Gearailt), <a href="/wiki/Fitzsimons" title="Fitzsimons">Fitzsimons</a> (Mac Síomóin/Mac an Ridire) and FitzHenry (Mac Anraí) – are descended from the initial Norman settlers. A small number of Irish families of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goidelic" title="Goidelic">Gaelic</a> origin came to use a Norman form of their original surname—so that Mac Giolla Phádraig became Fitzpatrick—while some assimilated so well that the Gaelic name was dropped in favor of a new, Hiberno-Norman form. Another common Irish surname of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiberno-Norman" title="Hiberno-Norman">Norman Irish</a> origin is the 'de' habitational prefix, meaning 'of' and originally signifying prestige and land ownership. Examples include de Búrca (Burke), de Brún, de Barra (Barry), de Stac (Stack), de Tiúit, de Faoite (White), de Londras (Landers), de Paor (Power). The Irish surname "Walsh" (in Gaelic <i>Breathnach</i>) was routinely given to settlers of <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a> origin, who had come during and after the Norman invasion. The Joyce and Griffin/Griffith (Gruffydd) families are also of Welsh origin.</p>
<p>The Mac Lochlainn, Ó Maol Seachlainn, Ó Maol Seachnaill, Ó Conchobhair, Mac Loughlin, Mac Diarmada, and Mac Loughlin families, all distinct, are now all subsumed together as MacLoughlin. The full surname usually indicated which family was in question, something that has been diminished with the loss of prefixes such as Ó and Mac. Different branches of a family with the same surname sometimes used distinguishing epithets, which sometimes became surnames in their own right. Hence the chief of the clan Ó Cearnaigh (Kearney) was referred to as An Sionnach (Fox), which his descendants use to this day. Similar surnames are often found in Scotland for many reasons, such as the use of a common language and mass Irish migration to Scotland in the late 19th and early to mid-20th centuries.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Medieval_and_Tudor_Ireland">Late Medieval and Tudor Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Late Medieval and Tudor Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galloglass-circa-1521.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2956" data-file-width="4000" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Galloglass-circa-1521.jpg/220px-Galloglass-circa-1521.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Galloglass-circa-1521.jpg/330px-Galloglass-circa-1521.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Galloglass-circa-1521.jpg/440px-Galloglass-circa-1521.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galloglass-circa-1521.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gaelic Irish soldiers in the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>, from a drawing of 1521 by <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Irish people of the Late Middle Ages were active as traders on the European continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-58">[58]</a></sup> They were distinguished from the English (who only used their own language or French) in that they only used <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> abroad—a language "spoken by all educated people throughout Gaeldom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> According to the writer <a href="/wiki/Seumas_MacManus" title="Seumas MacManus">Seumas MacManus</a>, the explorer <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> visited Ireland to gather information about the lands to the west,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacManus343_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacManus343-60">[60]</a></sup> a number of Irish names are recorded on Columbus' crew roster preserved in the archives of <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a> and it was an Irishman named Patrick Maguire who was the first to set foot in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> in 1492;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacManus343_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacManus343-60">[60]</a></sup> however, according to <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Eliot_Morison" title="Samuel Eliot Morison">Morison</a> and Miss Gould, who made a detailed study of the crew list of 1492, no Irish or English sailors were involved in the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>An English report of 1515 states that the Irish people were divided into over sixty Gaelic lordships and thirty Anglo-Irish lordships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> The English term for these lordships was "nation" or "country".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> The Irish term "<i>oireacht</i>" referred to both the territory and the people ruled by the lord.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> Literally, it meant an "assembly", where the <a href="/wiki/Brehon" title="Brehon">Brehons</a> would hold their courts upon hills to arbitrate the matters of the lordship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> Indeed, the Tudor lawyer <a href="/wiki/John_Davies_(poet)" title="John Davies (poet)">John Davies</a> described the Irish people with respect to their laws:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There is no people under the sun that doth love equal and indifferent (impartial) justice better than the Irish, or will rest better satisfied with the execution thereof, although it be against themselves, as they may have the protection and benefit of the law upon which just cause they do desire it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiref1_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-multiref1-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Another English commentator records that the assemblies were attended by "all the scum of the country"—the labouring population as well as the landowners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-6"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> While the distinction between "free" and "unfree" elements of the Irish people was unreal in legal terms, it was a social and economic reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-7"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> Social mobility was usually downwards, due to social and economic pressures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-8"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> The ruling clan's "expansion from the top downwards" was constantly displacing commoners and forcing them into the margins of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-9"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a clan-based society, <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a> was all important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-10"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> Ireland 'was justly styled a "Nation of Annalists"'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The various branches of Irish learning—including law, poetry, history and genealogy, and medicine—were associated with hereditary learned families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jefferies_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jefferies-64">[64]</a></sup> The poetic families included the <a href="/wiki/%C3%93_D%C3%A1laigh" title="Ó Dálaigh">Uí Dhálaigh</a> (Daly) and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=MacGrath_(disambiguation)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="MacGrath (disambiguation) (page does not exist)">MacGrath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholls_44-11"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls-44">[44]</a></sup> Irish physicians, such as the O'Briens in <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a> or the MacCailim Mor in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Isles" title="Western Isles">Western Isles</a>, were renowned in the courts of England, Spain, Portugal and the Low Countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiref1_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-multiref1-62">[62]</a></sup> Learning was not exclusive to the hereditary learned families, however; one such example is <a href="/wiki/Cathal_%C3%93g_Mac_Maghnusa" title="Cathal Óg Mac Maghnusa">Cathal Mac Manus</a>, the 15th century diocesan priest who wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/Annals_of_Ulster" title="Annals of Ulster">Annals of Ulster</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jefferies_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jefferies-64">[64]</a></sup> Other learned families included the <a href="/wiki/Mac_Aodhag%C3%A1in" title="Mac Aodhagáin">Mic Aodhagáin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clan_MacFhirbhisigh" title="Clan MacFhirbhisigh">Clann Fhir Bhisigh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jefferies_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jefferies-64">[64]</a></sup> It was this latter family which produced <a href="/wiki/Dubhaltach_Mac_Fhirbhisigh" title="Dubhaltach Mac Fhirbhisigh">Dubhaltach Mac Fhirbhisigh</a>, the 17th century genealogist and compiler of the <i><a href="/wiki/Leabhar_na_nGenealach" title="Leabhar na nGenealach">Leabhar na nGenealach</a></i>. (see also <a href="/wiki/Irish_medical_families" title="Irish medical families">Irish medical families</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plantations">Plantations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Plantations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plantations_of_Ireland" title="Plantations of Ireland">Plantations of Ireland</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulster-Scots" title="Ulster-Scots">Ulster-Scots</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Boyle_0001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="663" data-file-width="523" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Robert_Boyle_0001.jpg/220px-Robert_Boyle_0001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Robert_Boyle_0001.jpg/330px-Robert_Boyle_0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Robert_Boyle_0001.jpg/440px-Robert_Boyle_0001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Boyle_0001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>, Anglo-Irish scientist and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fathers_of_scientific_fields#Chemistry" title="Fathers of scientific fields">father of chemistry</a>, whose family obtained land in the plantations</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Ireland was subdued by England, the English—under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" title="James I of England">James I of England</a> (<abbr title="reign">r.</abbr> 1603–1625), the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a> <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> (1653–1658), <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William III of England</a> (<abbr title="reign">r.</abbr> 1689–1702) and their successors—began the settling of Protestant Scottish and English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonist" title="Colonist">colonists</a> into Ireland, where they settled most heavily in the northern <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Ireland" title="Provinces of Ireland">province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Plantations_of_Ireland" title="Plantations of Ireland">Plantations of Ireland</a>, and in particular the <a href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster" title="Plantation of Ulster">Plantation of Ulster</a> in the 17th century, introduced great numbers of Scottish, English as well as French <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenots</a> as colonists.</p>
<p>Many Gaelic Irish were displaced during the 17th century plantations. Only in the major part of Ulster did the plantations of mostly Scottish prove long-lived; the other three provinces (<a href="/wiki/Connacht" title="Connacht">Connacht</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a>) remained heavily <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> Irish. Eventually, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish" title="Anglo-Irish">Anglo-Irish</a> and Protestant populations of those three provinces decreased drastically as a result of the political developments in the early 20th century in Ireland, as well as the Catholic Church's <a href="/wiki/Ne_Temere" title="Ne Temere">Ne Temere</a> decree for mixed marriages, which obliged the non-Catholic partner to have the children raised as Catholics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enlightenment_Ireland">Enlightenment Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Enlightenment Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Irishmen" title="United Irishmen">United Irishmen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Callan" title="Nicholas Callan">Nicholas Callan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">Protestant Ascendancy</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(February 2010)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>There have been notable Irish scientists. The Anglo-Irish scientist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> (1627–1691) is considered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fathers_of_scientific_fields" title="Fathers of scientific fields">father</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> for his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sceptical_Chymist" title="The Sceptical Chymist">The Sceptical Chymist</a></i>, written in 1661.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Boyle was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomist" title="Atomist">atomist</a>, and is best known for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boyle%27s_Law" title="Boyle's Law">Boyle's Law</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrographer" title="Hydrographer">hydrographer</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rear_Admiral_(Royal_Navy)" title="Rear Admiral (Royal Navy)">Rear Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Francis_Beaufort" title="Francis Beaufort">Francis Beaufort</a> (1774–1857), an Irish naval officer of Huguenot descent, was the creator of the <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_scale" title="Beaufort scale">Beaufort scale</a> for indicating wind force. <a href="/wiki/George_Boole" title="George Boole">George Boole</a> (1815–1864), the mathematician who invented <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Boolean_algebra_(disambiguation)" title="Boolean algebra (disambiguation)">Boolean algebra</a>, spent the latter part of his life in <a href="/wiki/Cork_(city)" title="Cork (city)">Cork</a>. The 19th century physicist <a href="/wiki/George_Johnstone_Stoney" title="George Johnstone Stoney">George Stoney</a> introduced the idea and the name of the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>. He was the uncle of another notable physicist, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_FitzGerald" title="George FitzGerald">George FitzGerald</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jonathan_swift.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="375" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Jonathan_swift.jpg/220px-Jonathan_swift.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Jonathan_swift.jpg/330px-Jonathan_swift.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/Jonathan_swift.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jonathan_swift.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jonathan Swift, one of the foremost prose satirists in the English language</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Irish bardic system, along with the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic culture</a> and learned classes, were upset by the plantations, and went into decline. Among the last of the true bardic poets were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brian_Mac_Giolla_Ph%C3%A1draig" title="Brian Mac Giolla Phádraig">Brian Mac Giolla Phádraig</a> (c. 1580–1652) and <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1ibh%C3%AD_%C3%93_Bruadair" title="Dáibhí Ó Bruadair">Dáibhí Ó Bruadair</a> (1625–1698). The Irish poets of the late 17th and 18th centuries moved toward more modern dialects. Among the most prominent of this period were <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9amas_Dall_Mac_Cuarta" title="Séamas Dall Mac Cuarta">Séamas Dall Mac Cuarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peadar_%C3%93_Doirn%C3%ADn" title="Peadar Ó Doirnín">Peadar Ó Doirnín</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art_Mac_Cumhaigh" title="Art Mac Cumhaigh">Art Mac Cumhaigh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathal_Bu%C3%AD_Mac_Giolla_Ghunna" title="Cathal Buí Mac Giolla Ghunna">Cathal Buí Mac Giolla Ghunna</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_Cl%C3%A1rach_Mac_Domhnaill" title="Seán Clárach Mac Domhnaill">Seán Clárach Mac Domhnaill</a>. Irish Catholics continued to receive an education in secret "hedgeschools", in spite of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penal_laws" title="Penal laws">Penal laws</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> A knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> was common among the poor Irish mountaineers in the 17th century, who spoke it on special occasions, while cattle were bought and sold in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> in the mountain market-places of <a href="/wiki/County_Kerry" title="County Kerry">Kerry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>For a comparatively small population of about 6 million people, Ireland made an enormous contribution to literature during the enlightenment. <a href="/wiki/Irish_literature" title="Irish literature">Irish literature</a> encompasses the Irish and English languages. Notable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_fiction" title="Irish fiction">Irish writers</a> include <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a> (1667–1745), <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Sterne" title="Laurence Sterne">Laurence Sterne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Goldsmith" title="Oliver Goldsmith">Oliver Goldsmith</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bram_Stoker" title="Bram Stoker">Bram Stoker</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Irish_racism" title="Anti-Irish racism">Anti-Irish racism</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Great_Famine">The Great Famine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The Great Famine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Irish_Famine" title="Great Irish Famine">Great Irish Famine</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">

<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
